### Kube Controller Manager

- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Architecture
  - In this lecture we will talk about Kube Controller Manager.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 2 | **Type:** Architecture
  - As we discussed earlier, the Kube controller manager manages various controllers in Kubernetes.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 3 | **Type:** Concept
  - A controller is like an office or department within the master ship that have their own set of responsibilities, such as an office for the ships would be responsible for monitoring and taking necessary actions about the ships.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 4 | **Type:** Concept
  - Whenever a new ship arrives, or when a ship leaves or gets destroyed, another office could be one that manages the containers on the ships.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 5 | **Type:** Concept
  - They take care of containers that are damaged or fall off ships.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 6 | **Type:** Concept
  - So these offices are, number one, continuously on the lookout for the status of the ships, and two, takes necessary actions to remediate the situation.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 7 | **Type:** Concept
  - In the Kubernetes terms, a controller is a process that continuously monitors the state of various components within the system, and works towards bringing the whole system to the desired functioning state.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 8 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - For example, the node controller is responsible for monitoring the status of the notes and taking necessary actions to keep the applications running.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 9 | **Type:** Architecture
  - It does that through the cube API server.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 10 | **Type:** Concept
  - The node controller checks the status of the nodes every five seconds.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 11 | **Type:** Concept
  - That way the node controller can monitor the health of the nodes.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 12 | **Type:** Concept
  - If it stops receiving heartbeat from a node, the node is marked as unreachable, but it waits for 40s before marking it unreachable.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 13 | **Type:** Concept
  - After a node is marked unreachable, it gives it five minutes to come back up.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 14 | **Type:** Concept
  - If it doesn't, it removes the pods assigned to that node and provisions them on the healthy ones.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 15 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - If the pods are part of a replica set, the next controller is the replication controller.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 16 | **Type:** Concept
  - It is responsible for monitoring the status of replica sets, and ensuring that the desired number of pods are available at all times within the set.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 17 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - If a pod dies, it creates another one.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 18 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - Now those were just two examples of controllers.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 19 | **Type:** Concept
  - There are many more such controllers available within Kubernetes.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 20 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Whatever concepts we have seen so far in Kubernetes, such as deployments, services, namespaces, or persistent volumes and whatever intelligence is built into these constructs, it is implemented through these various controllers.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 21 | **Type:** Concept
  - As you can imagine, this is kind of the brain behind a lot of things in Kubernetes.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 22 | **Type:** Concept
  - Now, how do you see these controllers and where are they located in your cluster?
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 23 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - They are all packaged into a single process known as the Kubernetes Controller manager.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 24 | **Type:** Architecture
  - When you install the Kubernetes controller manager, the different controllers get installed as well.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 25 | **Type:** Architecture
  - So how do you install and view the Kubernetes Controller manager?
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 26 | **Type:** Architecture
  - Download the Kube controller manager from Kubernetes release page.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 27 | **Type:** Concept
  - Extract it and run it as a service.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 28 | **Type:** Concept
  - When you run it, as you can see, there are a list of options provided.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 29 | **Type:** Concept
  - This is where you provide additional options to customize your controller.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 30 | **Type:** Concept
  - Remember some of the default settings for node controller we discussed earlier, such as the node monitor period, the grace period, and the eviction timeout.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 31 | **Type:** Concept
  - These go in here as options.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 32 | **Type:** Concept
  - There is an additional option called controllers that you can use to specify which controllers to enable.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 33 | **Type:** Concept
  - By default, all of them are enabled, but you can choose to enable a select few.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 34 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - So in case any of your controllers don't seem to work or exist, this would be a good starting point to look at.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 35 | **Type:** Architecture
  - So how do you view the Kube Controller Manager server options?
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 36 | **Type:** Concept
  - Again, it depends on how you set up your cluster.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 37 | **Type:** Architecture
  - If you set it up with the kube admin tool, kube admin deploys the kube controller manager as a pod in the kube system namespace on the master node.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 38 | **Type:** Concept
  - You can see the options within the pod definition file located at Etsy Kubernetes manifest folder in a non kube admin setup.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 39 | **Type:** Architecture
  - You can inspect the options by viewing the Kube Controller Manager service located at the services directory.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 40 | **Type:** Architecture
  - You can also see the running process and the effective options by listing the process on the master node and searching for Kube Controller manager.
- **File:** `014_Kube Controller Manager.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 41 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Well, that's it for this lecture and I will see you in the next.

### Kube Scheduler

- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Architecture
  - In this lecture we will talk about kube scheduler.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 2 | **Type:** Architecture
  - Earlier we discussed that the Kubernetes Scheduler is responsible for scheduling pods and nodes.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 3 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - Now don't let the graphic mislead you.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 4 | **Type:** Architecture
  - Remember the scheduler is only responsible for deciding which pod goes on which node.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 5 | **Type:** Concept
  - It doesn't actually place the pod on the nodes.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 6 | **Type:** Architecture
  - That's the job of the kubelet.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 7 | **Type:** Architecture
  - The Kubelet or the captain on the ship is who creates the pod on the ships.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 8 | **Type:** Architecture
  - The scheduler only decides which pod goes where.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 9 | **Type:** Architecture
  - Let's look at how the scheduler does that in a bit more detail.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 10 | **Type:** Architecture
  - First of all, why do you need scheduler when there are many ships and many containers?
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 11 | **Type:** Concept
  - You want to make sure that the right container ends up on the right ship.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 12 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - For example, there could be different sizes of ships and containers.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 13 | **Type:** Concept
  - You want to make sure the ship has sufficient capacity to accommodate those containers.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 14 | **Type:** Concept
  - Different ships may be going to different destinations.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 15 | **Type:** Concept
  - You want to make sure your containers are placed on the right ships, so they end up in the right destination.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 16 | **Type:** Architecture
  - In Kubernetes, the scheduler decides which nodes the pods are placed on.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 17 | **Type:** Concept
  - Depending on certain criteria, you may have pods with different resource requirements.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 18 | **Type:** Concept
  - You can have nodes in the cluster dedicated to certain applications.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 19 | **Type:** Architecture
  - So how does the scheduler assign these pods?
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 20 | **Type:** Architecture
  - The scheduler looks at each pod and tries to find the best node for it.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 21 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - For example, let's take one of these pods, the big blue one.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 22 | **Type:** Concept
  - It has a set of CPU and memory requirements.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 23 | **Type:** Architecture
  - The scheduler goes through two phases to identify the best node for the pod.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 24 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - In the first phase, the scheduler tries to filter out the nodes that do not fit the profile for this pod.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 25 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - For example, the nodes that do not have sufficient CPU and memory resources requested by the pod.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 26 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So the first two nodes are filtered out.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 27 | **Type:** Concept
  - So we are now left with the two nodes on which the pod can be placed.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 28 | **Type:** Architecture
  - Now how does the scheduler pick one from the two?
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 29 | **Type:** Architecture
  - The scheduler ranks the nodes to identify the best fit for the pod.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 30 | **Type:** Concept
  - It uses a priority function to assign a score to the nodes on a scale of 0 to 10.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 31 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - For example, the scheduler calculates the amount of resources that would be free on the nodes after placing the pod on them.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 32 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this case, the one on the right would have six CPUs free if the pod was placed on it, which is four more than the other one.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 33 | **Type:** Concept
  - So it gets a better rank.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 34 | **Type:** Concept
  - And so it wins.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 35 | **Type:** Architecture
  - So that's how a scheduler works at a high level.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 36 | **Type:** Architecture
  - And of course these can be customized and you can write your own scheduler as well.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 37 | **Type:** Concept
  - There are many more topics to look at such as resource requirements, limits taints and tolerations.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 38 | **Type:** Concept
  - Node selectors, affinity rules, etc. which is why we have an entire section dedicated to scheduling coming up in this course, where we will discuss each of these in much more detail.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 39 | **Type:** Architecture
  - For now, we will continue to focus on the scheduler as a process at a high level.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 40 | **Type:** Architecture
  - So how do you install the scheduler?
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 41 | **Type:** Architecture
  - Download the kube scheduler binary from the Kubernetes release page, extract it and run it as a service.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 42 | **Type:** Architecture
  - When you run it as a service, you specify the scheduler configuration file.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 43 | **Type:** Architecture
  - So how do you view the kube scheduler server options?
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 44 | **Type:** Architecture
  - Again, if you set it up with the kube adm tool, the kube adm tool deploys the kube scheduler as a pod in the kube system namespace on the master node.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 45 | **Type:** Concept
  - You can see the options within the pod definition file located at Kubernetes manifest folder.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 46 | **Type:** Architecture
  - You can also see the running process and the effective options by listing the process on the master node and searching for kube scheduler.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 47 | **Type:** Concept
  - Well, that's it for this lecture.
- **File:** `015_Kube Scheduler.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 48 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - I will see you in the next.

### Kubelet

- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this lecture we will look at cubelet.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 2 | **Type:** Concept
  - Earlier we discussed that the Cubelet is like the captain of the ship.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 3 | **Type:** Concept
  - They lead all activities on a ship.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 4 | **Type:** Concept
  - They are the ones responsible for doing all the paperwork necessary to become part of the cluster.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 5 | **Type:** Concept
  - They are the sole point of contact from the master ship.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 6 | **Type:** Architecture
  - They load or unload containers on the ship as instructed by the scheduler on the master.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 7 | **Type:** Concept
  - They also send back reports at regular intervals on the status of the ship and the containers on them.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 8 | **Type:** Concept
  - The cubelet in the Kubernetes worker node registers the node with the Kubernetes cluster.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 9 | **Type:** Operational Insight
  - When it receives instructions to load a container or a pod on the node, it requests the container runtime engine, which may be Docker, to pull the required image and run an instance.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 10 | **Type:** Architecture
  - The Cubelet then continues to monitor the state of the pod and containers in it, and reports to the kube API server on a timely basis.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 11 | **Type:** Concept
  - So how do you install the cubelet?
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 12 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - If you use the kube admin tool to deploy your cluster, it does not automatically deploy the Cubelet.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 13 | **Type:** Comparison
  - Now that's the difference from the other components.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 14 | **Type:** Architecture
  - You must always manually install the Kubelet on your worker nodes.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 15 | **Type:** Concept
  - Download the installer, extract it and run it as a service.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 16 | **Type:** Concept
  - You can view the running cubelet process and the effective options by listing the process on the worker node and searching for Cubelet.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 17 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - We will look more into Cubelets, how to configure Cubelets, generate certificates, and finally how to TLS bootstrap Cubelets later in this course.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 18 | **Type:** Concept
  - That's it for now.
- **File:** `016_Kubelet.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 19 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - I will see you in the next lecture.

### Kube Proxy

- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this lecture we will talk about kube proxy within a Kubernetes cluster.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 2 | **Type:** Concept
  - Every pod can reach every other pod.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 3 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - This is accomplished by deploying a pod networking solution to the cluster.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 4 | **Type:** Concept
  - A pod network is an internal virtual network that spans across all the nodes in the cluster, to which all the pods connect to.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 5 | **Type:** Concept
  - Through this network, they're able to communicate with each other.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 6 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - There are many solutions available for deploying such a network.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 7 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - In this case, I have a web application deployed on the first node and a database application deployed on the second.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 8 | **Type:** Concept
  - The web app can reach the database simply by using the IP of the pod, but there is no guarantee that the IP of the database pod will always remain the same.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 9 | **Type:** Concept
  - If you've gone through the lecture on services as discussed in the beginner's course, you must know that a better way for the web application to access the database is using a service.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 10 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So we create a service to expose the database application across the cluster.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 11 | **Type:** Concept
  - The web application can now access the database using the name of the service DB.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 12 | **Type:** Concept
  - The service also gets an IP address assigned to it whenever a pod tries to reach the surface using its IP or name, it forwards the traffic to the backend pod.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 13 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this case, the database.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 14 | **Type:** Concept
  - But what is this service and how does it get an IP?
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 15 | **Type:** Concept
  - Does the service join the same pod network?
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 16 | **Type:** Concept
  - The service cannot join the pod network because the service is not an actual thing.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 17 | **Type:** Concept
  - It is not a container like pods, so it doesn't have any interfaces or an actively listening process.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 18 | **Type:** Concept
  - It is a virtual component that only lives in the Kubernetes memory.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 19 | **Type:** Best Practice
  - But then we also said that the service should be accessible across the cluster from any nodes.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 20 | **Type:** Concept
  - So how is that achieved?
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 21 | **Type:** Concept
  - That's where kube proxy comes in.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 22 | **Type:** Concept
  - Kube proxy is a process that runs on each node in the Kubernetes cluster.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 23 | **Type:** Concept
  - Its job is to look for new services.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 24 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - And every time a new service is created, it creates the appropriate rules on each node to forward traffic to those services to the backend pods.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 25 | **Type:** Concept
  - One way it does this is using iptables rules.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 26 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - In this case, it creates an iptables rule on each node in the cluster to forward traffic heading to the IP of the service, which is 10.96.0.1.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 27 | **Type:** Concept
  - To the IP of the actual port, which is 10.32.0.15.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 28 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So that's how kube proxy configures a service.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 29 | **Type:** Concept
  - We discussed a lot more about networking and services and kube proxy and pod networking later in this course.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 30 | **Type:** Concept
  - Again we have a large section just for networking.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 31 | **Type:** Concept
  - This is a high level overview for now.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 32 | **Type:** Concept
  - We will now see how to install kube proxy.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 33 | **Type:** Concept
  - Download the kube proxy binary from the Kubernetes release page.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 34 | **Type:** Concept
  - Extract it and run it as a service.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 35 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The kube adm tool deploys kube proxy as pods on each node.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 36 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - In fact, it is deployed as a daemonset, so a single pod is always deployed on each node in the cluster.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 37 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - Well, if you don't know about Daemonset yet, don't worry.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 38 | **Type:** Concept
  - We have a lecture on that coming up in this course.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 39 | **Type:** Concept
  - We have now covered a high level overview of the Kubernetes components, including control plane and node level components like kube proxy.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 40 | **Type:** Concept
  - As mentioned, we will look at some of these in much more detail at various sections in this course.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 41 | **Type:** Concept
  - Well that's it for this lecture.
- **File:** `017_Kube Proxy.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 42 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - I will see you in the next section.

### Pods

- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Concept
  - Before we head into understanding pods, we would like to assume that the following have been set up already.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 2 | **Type:** Concept
  - At this point, we assume that the application is already developed and built into Docker images, and it is available on Docker repository like Docker Hub, so Kubernetes can pull it down.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 3 | **Type:** Concept
  - We also assume that the Kubernetes cluster has already been set up and is working.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 4 | **Type:** Concept
  - This could be a single node setup or a multi node setup.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 5 | **Type:** Concept
  - Doesn't matter.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 6 | **Type:** Concept
  - All the services need to be in a running state, as we discussed before with Kubernetes.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 7 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Our ultimate aim is to deploy our application in the form of containers on a set of machines that are configured as worker nodes in a cluster.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 8 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - However, Kubernetes does not deploy containers directly on the worker nodes.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 9 | **Type:** Concept
  - The containers are encapsulated into a Kubernetes object known as pods.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 10 | **Type:** Concept
  - A pod is a single instance of an application.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 11 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - A pod is the smallest object that you can create in Kubernetes.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 12 | **Type:** Concept
  - Here we see the simplest of simplest cases, where you have a single node Kubernetes cluster with a single instance of your application running in a single Docker container encapsulated in a pod.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 13 | **Type:** Concept
  - What if the number of users accessing your application increase and you need to scale your application?
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 14 | **Type:** Concept
  - You need to add additional instances of your web application to share the load.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 15 | **Type:** Concept
  - Now where would you spin up additional instances?
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 16 | **Type:** Concept
  - Do we bring up new container instances within the same pod?
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 17 | **Type:** Concept
  - No.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 18 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - We create new pod altogether with a new instance of the same application.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 19 | **Type:** Concept
  - As you can see, we now have two instances of our web application running on two separate paths on the same Kubernetes system or node.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 20 | **Type:** Concept
  - What if the user base further increases and your current node has no sufficient capacity?
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 21 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Well, then you can always deploy additional pods on a new node in the cluster.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 22 | **Type:** Concept
  - You will have a new node added to the cluster to expand the cluster's physical capacity.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 23 | **Type:** Concept
  - So what I'm trying to illustrate in this slide is that pods usually have a 1 to 1 relationship, with containers running your application.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 24 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - To scale up, you create new pods, and to scale down you delete existing pods.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 25 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - You do not add additional containers to an existing pod to scale your application.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 26 | **Type:** Concept
  - Also, if you're wondering how we implement all of this and how we achieve load balancing between containers, etc., we will get into all of that in a later lecture.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 27 | **Type:** Concept
  - For now, we are only trying to understand the basic concepts.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 28 | **Type:** Concept
  - We just said that pods usually have a 1 to 1 relationship with the containers.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 29 | **Type:** Concept
  - But are we restricted to having a single container in a single pod?
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 30 | **Type:** Concept
  - No.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 31 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - A single pod can have multiple containers, except for the fact that they're usually not multiple containers of the same kind.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 32 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - As we discussed in the previous slide, if our intention was to scale our application, then we would need to create additional pods.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 33 | **Type:** Concept
  - But sometimes you might have a scenario where you have a helper container that might be doing some kind of supporting task for our web application, such as processing a user entered data, processing a file uploaded by the user, etc. and you want t...
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 34 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - In that case, you can have both of these containers part of the same pod, so that when a new application container is created.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 35 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The helper is also created and when it dies, the helper also dies since they are part of the same pod.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 36 | **Type:** Concept
  - The two containers can also communicate with each other directly by referring to each other as localhost, since they share the same network space.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 37 | **Type:** Concept
  - Plus, they can easily share the same storage space as well.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 38 | **Type:** Concept
  - If you still have doubts in this topic, I would understand if you did, because I did.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 39 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The first time I learned these concepts, we could take another shot at understanding pods from a different angle.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 40 | **Type:** Concept
  - Let's for a moment keep Kubernetes out of our discussion and talk about simple Docker containers.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 41 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Let's assume we were developing a process or a script to deploy our application on a Docker host.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 42 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Then we would first simply deploy our application using a simple Docker run Python app command.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 43 | **Type:** Concept
  - And the application runs fine and our users are able to access it.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 44 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - When the load increases, we deploy more instances of our application by running the Docker run commands many more times.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 45 | **Type:** Concept
  - This works fine and we're all happy.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 46 | **Type:** Concept
  - Now, sometime in the future, our application is further developed, undergoes architectural changes, and grows and gets complex.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 47 | **Type:** Concept
  - We now have a new helper container that helps our web application by processing or fetching data from elsewhere.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 48 | **Type:** Concept
  - These helper containers maintain a 1 to 1 relationship with our application container, and thus needs to communicate with the application containers directly and access data from those containers.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 49 | **Type:** Concept
  - For this, we need to maintain a map of what app and helper containers are connected to each other.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 50 | **Type:** Concept
  - We would need to establish network connectivity between these containers ourselves using links and custom networks.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 51 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - We would need to create sharable volumes and share it among the containers.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 52 | **Type:** Concept
  - We would need to maintain a map of that as well.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 53 | **Type:** Concept
  - And most importantly, we would need to monitor the state of the application container and when it dies, manually kill the helper container as well, as it's no longer required.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 54 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - When a new container is deployed, we would need to deploy the new helper container as well with pods.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 55 | **Type:** Concept
  - Kubernetes does all of this for us automatically.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 56 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - We just need to define what containers a pod consists of, and the containers in a pod by default will have access to the same storage, the same network namespace, and same fate as in they will be created together and destroyed together.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 57 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Even if our application didn't happen to be so complex and we could live with a single container, Kubernetes still requires you to create pods, but this is good in the long run as your application is now equipped for architectural changes and scal...
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 58 | **Type:** Concept
  - However, also note that Multi-container pod are a rare use case, and we're going to stick to single containers per pod in this course.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 59 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Let us now look at how to deploy pods.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 60 | **Type:** Concept
  - Earlier we learned about the Kube control run command.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 61 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - What this command really does is it deploys a Docker container by creating a pod.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 62 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So it first creates a pod automatically and deploys an instance of the nginx docker image.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 63 | **Type:** Concept
  - But where does it get the application image from?
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 64 | **Type:** Concept
  - For that you need to specify the image name using the image parameter.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 65 | **Type:** Concept
  - The application image.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 66 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this case the nginx image is downloaded from the Docker Hub repository.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 67 | **Type:** Command
  - 
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 68 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - You could configure Kubernetes to pull the image from the public Docker hub or a private repository within the The organization.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 69 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Now that we have a pod created, how do we see the list of pods available?
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 70 | **Type:** Concept
  - The Kube control get pods command helps us see the list of pods in our cluster.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 71 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this case, we see the pod is in a container creating state and soon changes to a running state when it is actually running.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 72 | **Type:** Concept
  - Also, remember that we haven't really talked about the concepts on how a user can access the nginx web server, and so in the current state, we haven't made the web server accessible to external users.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 73 | **Type:** Operational Insight
  - You can access it internally from the node.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 74 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - But for now we will just see how to deploy a pod.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 75 | **Type:** Concept
  - And later in a later lecture, once we learn about networking and services, we will get to know how to make this service accessible to end users.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 76 | **Type:** Concept
  - Well, that's it for this lecture.
- **File:** `018_Pods.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 77 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Head over to a demo and I will see you in the next one.

### Pods with YAML

- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this lecture we will talk about creating a pod using a YAML based configuration file.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 2 | **Type:** Concept
  - In the previous lecture we learned about YAML files in general.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 3 | **Type:** Concept
  - Now we will learn how to develop YAML files specifically for Kubernetes.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 4 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Kubernetes uses YAML files as inputs for the creation of objects such as pods, replicas, deployments, services, etc. all of these follow a similar structure.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 5 | **Type:** Concept
  - A Kubernetes definition file always contains four top level fields the API version, kind, metadata, and spec.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 6 | **Type:** Concept
  - These are the top level or root level properties.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 7 | **Type:** Concept
  - These are also required fields, so you must have them in your configuration file.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 8 | **Type:** Concept
  - Let us look at each one of them.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 9 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The first one is the API version.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 10 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - This is the version of the Kubernetes API we are using to create the object, depending on what we are trying to create.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 11 | **Type:** Concept
  - We must use the right API version for now since we are working on pods.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 12 | **Type:** Concept
  - We will set the API version as v1.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 13 | **Type:** Concept
  - Few other possible values for this field are apps v1, beta, extensions v1, beta, etc. we will see what these are for later in this course.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 14 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Next is the kind.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 15 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The kind refers to the type of object we are trying to create, which in this case happens to be a pod.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 16 | **Type:** Concept
  - So we will set it as pod.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 17 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Some other possible values here could be replica set or deployment or service, which is what you see in the kind field in the table on the right.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 18 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The next is metadata.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 19 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The metadata is data about the object like its name, labels, etc. as you can see, unlike the first two where you have specified a string value, this is in the form of a dictionary.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 20 | **Type:** Concept
  - So everything under metadata is intended to the right a little bit.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 21 | **Type:** Concept
  - And so names and labels are children of metadata.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 22 | **Type:** Best Practice
  - The number of spaces before the two properties, name and labels doesn't matter, but they should be the same as they are siblings.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 23 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this case, labels has more spaces on the left than name, and so it is now a child of the name property instead of a sibling, which is incorrect.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 24 | **Type:** Concept
  - Also, the two properties must have more spaces than its parent, which is metadata, so that it's intended to the right a little bit.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 25 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this case, all three of them have the same number of spaces before them, and so they are all siblings, which is not correct under metadata.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 26 | **Type:** Concept
  - The name is a string value.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 27 | **Type:** Concept
  - So you can name your pod my app pod and the labels is a dictionary.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 28 | **Type:** Concept
  - So labels is a dictionary within the metadata dictionary.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 29 | **Type:** Concept
  - And it can have any key and value pairs as you wish.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 30 | **Type:** Concept
  - For now I have added a label app with the value My app.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 31 | **Type:** Concept
  - Similarly, you could add other labels as you see fit, which will help you identify these objects at a later point in time.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 32 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - Say, for example, there are hundreds of pods running a front end application and hundreds of pods running a back end application or a database.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 33 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - It will be difficult for you to group these pods once they are deployed.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 34 | **Type:** Concept
  - If you label them now as front end, back end, or database, you will be able to filter the pods based on this label at a later point in time.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 35 | **Type:** Concept
  - It's important to note that under metadata, you can only specify name or labels or anything else that Kubernetes expects to be under metadata.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 36 | **Type:** Concept
  - You cannot add any other property as you wish under this.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 37 | **Type:** Concept
  - However, under labels you can have any kind of key or value pairs as you see fit.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 38 | **Type:** Concept
  - So it's important to understand what each of these parameters expect.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 39 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So far, we have only mentioned the type and name of the object we need to create, which happens to be a pod with a name Myapp pod, but we haven't really specified the Divide the container or image we need in the pod.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 40 | **Type:** Concept
  - The last section in the configuration file is the specification section, which is written as spec.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 41 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Depending on the object we are going to create.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 42 | **Type:** Concept
  - This is where we would provide additional information to Kubernetes pertaining to that object.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 43 | **Type:** Concept
  - This is going to be different for different objects.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 44 | **Type:** Concept
  - So it's important to understand or refer to the documentation section to get the right format for each.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 45 | **Type:** Concept
  - Since we are only creating a pod with a single container in it, it is easy.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 46 | **Type:** Concept
  - Spec is a dictionary, so add a property under it called containers.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 47 | **Type:** Concept
  - Containers is a list or an array.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 48 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - The reason this property is a list is because the pods can have multiple containers within them, as we learned in the lecture earlier.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 49 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this case though, we will only add a single item in the list since we plan to have only a single container in the pod.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 50 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The dash right before the name indicates that this is the first item in the list.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 51 | **Type:** Concept
  - The item in the list is a dictionary, so add a name and image property.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 52 | **Type:** Concept
  - The value for image is nginx, which is the name of the Docker image in the Docker repository.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 53 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Once the file is created, run the command kubectl create dash f followed by the file name, which is pod definition YAML and Kubernetes creates the pod.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 54 | **Type:** Concept
  - So to summarize, remember the four top level properties API version kind, metadata and spec.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 55 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Then start by adding values to those depending on the object you are going to create.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 56 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Once we create the pod, how do you see it?
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 57 | **Type:** Concept
  - Use the kubectl get pods command to see a list of pods available.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 58 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this case it's just one.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 59 | **Type:** Concept
  - To see detailed information about the pod, run the kubectl describe pod command.
- **File:** `019_Pods with YAML.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 60 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - This will tell you information about the pod when it was created, what labels are assigned to it, what Docker containers are part of it, and the events associated with that pod.

- **File:** `025_Lab Solution - Pods (optional).extraction.md` | **Entry:** 75 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So run check, all right, okay, so that's the end of the lab, let's hit the End Lab button, and I'll see you in the next one.

### Recap - ReplicaSets

- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Concept
  - Controllers are the brain behind Kubernetes.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 2 | **Type:** Concept
  - They are the processes that monitor Kubernetes objects and respond accordingly.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 3 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this lecture, we will discuss about one controller in particular and that is the replication controller.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 4 | **Type:** Concept
  - So what is a replica and why do we need a replication controller?
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 5 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Let's go back to our first scenario where we had a single pod running our application.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 6 | **Type:** Troubleshooting
  - What if for some reason our application crashes and the pod fails, users will no longer be able to access our application?
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 7 | **Type:** Concept
  - To prevent users from losing access to our application, we would like to have more than one instance or pod running at the same time.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 8 | **Type:** Troubleshooting
  - That way, if one fails, we still have our application running on the other one.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 9 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - The replication controller helps us run multiple instances of a single pod in the Kubernetes cluster, Thus providing high availability.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 10 | **Type:** Concept
  - So does that mean you can't use a replication controller if you plan to have a single pod?
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 11 | **Type:** Concept
  - No.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 12 | **Type:** Troubleshooting
  - Even if you have a single pod, replication controller can help by automatically bringing up a new pod when the existing one fails.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 13 | **Type:** Concept
  - Thus, the replication controller ensures that the specified number of pods are running at all times, even if it's just 1 or 100.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 14 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - Another reason we need replication controller is to create multiple pods to share the load across them.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 15 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - For example, in this simple scenario, we have a single pod serving a set of users.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 16 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - When the number of users increased, we deploy additional pod to balance the load across the two pods.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 17 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - If the demand further increases, and if we were to run out of resources on the first node, we could deploy additional pods across the other nodes in the cluster.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 18 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - As you can see, the replication controller spans across multiple nodes in the cluster.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 19 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - It helps us balance the load across multiple pods on different nodes, as well as scale our application when the demand increases.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 20 | **Type:** Concept
  - It's important to note that there are two similar terms replication controller and replica set.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 21 | **Type:** Concept
  - Both have the same purpose, but they are not the same.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 22 | **Type:** Concept
  - Replication controller is the older technology that is being replaced by replica set.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 23 | **Type:** Best Practice
  - Replica set is the new recommended way to set up replication.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 24 | **Type:** Concept
  - However, whatever we discussed in the previous few slides remain applicable to both these technologies.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 25 | **Type:** Comparison
  - There are minor differences in the way each works, and we will look at that in a bit.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 26 | **Type:** Concept
  - As such, we will try to stick to replica sets in all of our demos and implementations going forward.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 27 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So let us now look at how we create a replication controller.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 28 | **Type:** Concept
  - As with the previous lecture, we start by creating a replication controller definition file.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 29 | **Type:** Concept
  - We will name it rc definition dot YAML.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 30 | **Type:** Concept
  - As with any Kubernetes definition file, we have four sections the API, version, kind, metadata, and spec.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 31 | **Type:** Concept
  - The API version is specific to what we are creating.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 32 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this case, replication controller is supported in Kubernetes API version v1.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 33 | **Type:** Concept
  - So we will set it as v1.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 34 | **Type:** Concept
  - The kind as we know is replication controller.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 35 | **Type:** Concept
  - Under metadata we will add a name and we will call it Myapp dash RC.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 36 | **Type:** Concept
  - And we will also add a few labels app and type and assign values to them.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 37 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So far, it has been very similar to how we created a pod in the previous section.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 38 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The next is the most crucial part of the definition file, and that is the specification written as spec for any Kubernetes definition file.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 39 | **Type:** Concept
  - The spec section defines what's inside the object we are creating.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 40 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - In this case, we know that the replication controller creates multiple instances of a pod.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 41 | **Type:** Concept
  - But what pod?
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 42 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - We create a template section under spec to provide a pod template to be used by the replication controller to create replicas.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 43 | **Type:** Concept
  - Now how do we define the pod template?
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 44 | **Type:** Concept
  - It's not that hard because we have already done that in the previous exercise.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 45 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Remember we created a Pod definition file in the previous exercise, we could reuse the contents of the file.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 46 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - To populate the templates section, move all the contents of the Pod definition file into the template section of the replication controller, except for the first few lines which are API, version and kind.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 47 | **Type:** Best Practice
  - Remember, whatever we move must be under the templates section, meaning they should be intended to the right and have more spaces before them than the template line itself.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 48 | **Type:** Best Practice
  - They should be children of the template section.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 49 | **Type:** Concept
  - Looking at our file now, we now have two metadata sections.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 50 | **Type:** Concept
  - One is for the replication controller and another for the pod.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 51 | **Type:** Concept
  - And we have two spec sections, one for each.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 52 | **Type:** Concept
  - We have nested two definition files together, the replication controller being the parent and the pod definition being the child.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 53 | **Type:** Concept
  - Now there is something still missing.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 54 | **Type:** Concept
  - We haven't mentioned how many replicas we need in the replication controller.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 55 | **Type:** Concept
  - For that, add another property to the spec called replicas and input the number of replicas you need under it.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 56 | **Type:** Concept
  - Remember that the template and replicas are direct children of spec sections, so they are siblings and must be on the same vertical line, which means having equal number of spaces before them.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 57 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Once the file is ready, run the kubectl create command and input the file using the f parameter.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 58 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The replication controller is created when the replication controller is created.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 59 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - It first creates the pods using the pod definition template as many as required, which is three in this case.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 60 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - To view the list of created replication controllers, run the kubectl get replication controller command and you will see the replication controller listed.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 61 | **Type:** Concept
  - We can also see the desired number of replicas or pods, the current number of replicas, and how many of them are ready in the output.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 62 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - If you would like to see the pods that were created by the replication controller, run the kubectl get pods command and you will see three pods running.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 63 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Note that all of them are starting with the name of the replication controller, which is my app RC, indicating that they are all created automatically by the replication controller.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 64 | **Type:** Concept
  - What we just saw was the replication controller.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 65 | **Type:** Concept
  - Let us now look at replica set.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 66 | **Type:** Concept
  - It is very similar to replication controller as usual.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 67 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - First we have API version kind metadata and spec.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 68 | **Type:** Concept
  - The API version though is a bit different.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 69 | **Type:** Concept
  - It is apps slash v1, which is different from what we had before.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 70 | **Type:** Concept
  - For replication controller which was just v1.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 71 | **Type:** Troubleshooting
  - If you get this wrong, you are likely to get an error that looks like this.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 72 | **Type:** Concept
  - It would say no match for kind replica set, because the specified Kubernetes API version has no support for replica set.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 73 | **Type:** Concept
  - The kind would be replica set and we add name and labels in the metadata.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 74 | **Type:** Concept
  - The specification section looks very similar to Replication controller.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 75 | **Type:** Concept
  - It has a template section where we provide pod definition as before.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 76 | **Type:** Concept
  - So I'm going to copy contents over from pod definition file.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 77 | **Type:** Concept
  - And we have number of replicas which is set to three.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 78 | **Type:** Comparison
  - However there is one major difference between replication controller and replica set.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 79 | **Type:** Concept
  - Replica set requires a selector definition.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 80 | **Type:** Concept
  - The selector section helps the replica set identify what pods fall under it, but why would you have to specify what pods fall under it if you have provided the contents of the pod definition file itself in the template.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 81 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - It's because replica set can also manage pods that were not created as part of the replica set creation.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 82 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - Say for example, there were pods created before the creation of the replica set that matched labels specified in the selector.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 83 | **Type:** Concept
  - The replica set will also take those pods into consideration when creating the replicas.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 84 | **Type:** Comparison
  - I will elaborate this in the next slide, but before we get into that, I would like to mention that the selector is one of the major differences between replication controller and replica set.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 85 | **Type:** Concept
  - The selector is not a required field in case of a replication controller, but it is still available when you skip it, as we did in the previous slide.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 86 | **Type:** Concept
  - It assumes it to be the same as the labels provided in the Pod definition file.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 87 | **Type:** Concept
  - In case of replica set, a user input is required for this property, and it has to be written in the form of match labels as shown here.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 88 | **Type:** Concept
  - The match labels selector simply matches the labels specified under it to the labels on the pods.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 89 | **Type:** Concept
  - The replica set selector also provides many other options for matching labels that were not available in a replication controller.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 90 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - And as always, to create a replica set, run the kubectl create command, providing the definition file as input.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 91 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - And to see the created replicas run the kubectl get replica set command to get list of pods.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 92 | **Type:** Concept
  - Simply run the kube control get pods command.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 93 | **Type:** Concept
  - So what is the deal with labels and selectors?
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 94 | **Type:** Concept
  - Why do we label our pods and objects in Kubernetes?
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 95 | **Type:** Concept
  - Let us look at a simple scenario.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 96 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Say we deployed three instances of our front end web application has three parts.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 97 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - We would like to create a replication controller or replica set to ensure that we have three active pods at any time.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 98 | **Type:** Concept
  - And yes, that is one of the use case of replica sets.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 99 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - You can use it to monitor existing pods if you have them already created as it is in this example.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 100 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - In case they were not created, the replica set will create them for you.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 101 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The role of the replica set is to monitor the pods and if any of them were to fail, deploy new ones.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 102 | **Type:** Concept
  - The replica set is in fact a process that monitors the pods.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 103 | **Type:** Concept
  - Now, how does the replica set know what pods to monitor?
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 104 | **Type:** Concept
  - There could be hundreds of other pods in the cluster running different applications.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 105 | **Type:** Concept
  - This is where labeling our pods during creation comes in handy.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 106 | **Type:** Concept
  - We could now provide these labels as a filter for replica set under the selector section, we use the Match Labels filter and provide the same label that we used while creating the pods.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 107 | **Type:** Concept
  - This way, the replica set knows which pods to monitor.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 108 | **Type:** Concept
  - The same concept of labels and selectors is used in many other places throughout Kubernetes.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 109 | **Type:** Concept
  - Now let me ask you a question along the same lines.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 110 | **Type:** Concept
  - In the replica set specification section, we learned that there are three sections template, replicas, and selector.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 111 | **Type:** Concept
  - We need three replicas and we have updated our selector based on our discussion in the previous slide.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 112 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Say for instance, we have the same scenario as in the previous slide where we have three existing parts that were created already.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 113 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - And we need to create a replica set to monitor the pods to ensure there are a minimum of three running at all times.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 114 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - When the replication controller is created, it is not going to deploy a new instance of Pod.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 115 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - As three of them with matching labels are already created.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 116 | **Type:** Concept
  - In that case, do we really need to provide a template session in the replica set specification?
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 117 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Since we are not expecting the replica set to create a new pod on deployment?
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 118 | **Type:** Concept
  - Yes we do.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 119 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Because in case one of the pods were to fail in the future, the replica set needs to create a new one to maintain the desired number of pods.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 120 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - And for the replica set to create a new pod, the template definition section is required.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 121 | **Type:** Concept
  - Let's now look at how we scale the replica set.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 122 | **Type:** Concept
  - Say we started with three replicas and the future.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 123 | **Type:** Concept
  - We decided to scale to six.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 124 | **Type:** Concept
  - How do we update our replica set to scale to six replicas?
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 125 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - Well, there are multiple ways to do it.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 126 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The first is to update the number of replicas in the definition file to six.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 127 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Then run the kubectl replace command to specify the same file using the f parameter, and that will update the replica set to have six replicas.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 128 | **Type:** Concept
  - The second way to do it is to run the kube control scale command.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 129 | **Type:** Concept
  - Use the replicas parameter to provide the new number of replicas and specify the same file as input.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 130 | **Type:** Concept
  - You may either input the definition file or provide the replica set name in the type name format.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 131 | **Type:** Concept
  - However, remember that using the file name as input will not result in the number of replicas being updated automatically in the file.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 132 | **Type:** Concept
  - In other words, the number of replicas in the replica set definition file will still be three, even though you scaled your replica set to have six replicas using the Kube control scale command and the file as input.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 133 | **Type:** Concept
  - There are also options available for automatically scaling the replica set based on load, but that is an advanced topic and we will discuss it at a later time.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 134 | **Type:** Concept
  - Let us now review the commands real quick.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 135 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The cube control create command as we know, is used to create a replica, set or basically any object in Kubernetes depending on the file we are providing as input.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 136 | **Type:** Concept
  - You must provide the input file using the dash f parameter.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 137 | **Type:** Concept
  - Use the kube control.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 138 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Get command to see the list of replica sets created.
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 139 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Use the cube control delete replica set command followed by the name of the replica set to delete the replica set, and then we have the control replace command to replace or update the replica set, and also the Kube control scale command to scale ...
- **File:** `026_Recap - ReplicaSets.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 140 | **Type:** Concept
  - That's it for this lecture.

### Lab Solution - ReplicaSets (optional)

- **File:** `028_Lab Solution - ReplicaSets (optional).extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Concept
  - -: Okay, so, in this video we're gonna go over the solution for the lab on ReplicaSets.
- **File:** `028_Lab Solution - ReplicaSets (optional).extraction.md` | **Entry:** 2 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So, let's start with the first question.
- **File:** `028_Lab Solution - ReplicaSets (optional).extraction.md` | **Entry:** 3 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So, the first question is, how many pods exist on the system?
- **File:** `028_Lab Solution - ReplicaSets (optional).extraction.md` | **Entry:** 4 | **Type:** Concept
  - So, just like before, we are on the Kubectl get pods command and there are no pods at the moment, so select zero.
- **File:** `028_Lab Solution - ReplicaSets (optional).extraction.md` | **Entry:** 5 | **Type:** Implementation Step
