### Deployments

- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - In this lecture we will discuss about Kubernetes deployments.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 2 | **Type:** Concept
  - For a minute.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 3 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Let us forget about pods and replica sets and other Kubernetes concepts, and talk about how you might want to deploy your application in a production environment.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 4 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - Say, for example, you have a web server that needs to be deployed in a production environment.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 5 | **Type:** Concept
  - You need not one, but many such instances of the web server running for obvious reasons.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 6 | **Type:** Concept
  - Secondly, whenever newer versions of application builds become available on the Docker registry, you would like to upgrade your Docker instances seamlessly.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 7 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - However, when you upgrade your instances, you do not want to upgrade all of them at once, as we just did.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 8 | **Type:** Concept
  - This may impact users accessing our applications, so you might want to upgrade them one after the other.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 9 | **Type:** Concept
  - And that kind of upgrade is known as rolling updates.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 10 | **Type:** Troubleshooting
  - Suppose one of the upgrades you performed resulted in an unexpected error, and you're asked to undo the recent change You would like to be able to roll back the changes that were recently carried out.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 11 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - Finally, say, for example, you would like to make multiple changes to your environment, such as upgrading the underlying web server versions, as well as scaling your environment and also modifying the resource allocations, etc. you do not want to ...
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 12 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Instead, you would like to apply a path to your environment, make the changes and then resume so that all the changes are rolled out together.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 13 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - All of these capabilities are available with the Kubernetes deployments.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 14 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So far in this course, we discussed about pods, which deploy single instances of our application, such as the web application.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 15 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this case, each container is encapsulated in pods.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 16 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - Multiple such pods are deployed using replication controllers or replica sets.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 17 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - And then comes deployment, which is a Kubernetes object that comes higher in the hierarchy.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 18 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The deployment provides us with the capability to upgrade the underlying instances seamlessly using rolling updates, undo changes, and pause and resume changes as required.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 19 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So how do we create a deployment?
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 20 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - As with the previous components, we first create a deployment definition file.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 21 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The contents of the deployment definition file are exactly similar to the replica set definition file, except for the kind which is now going to be deployment.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 22 | **Type:** Concept
  - If we walk through the contents of the file, it has an API version which is apps forward slash v1 metadata, which has name and labels, and a spec that has template replicas and selector.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 23 | **Type:** Concept
  - The template has a pod definition inside it.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 24 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Once the file is ready, run the kube control create command and specify the deployment definition file.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 25 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Then run the kube control.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 26 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Get deployments command to see the newly created deployment.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 27 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The deployment automatically creates a replica set.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 28 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So if you run the kube control get replica set command, you will be able to see a new replica set in the name of the deployment.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 29 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The replica sets ultimately create paths, so if you run the cube Control get Pods command, you will be able to see the pods with the name of the deployment and the replica set.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 30 | **Type:** Comparison
  - So far, there hasn't been much of a difference between replica set and deployments, except for the fact that deployments created a new Kubernetes object called deployments.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 31 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - We will see how to take advantage of the deployment using the use cases we discussed in the previous slide in the upcoming lectures.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 32 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - And one more note before we end this lecture to see all the created objects at once, run the cube control get all command.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 33 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - And in this case we can see that the deployment was created.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 34 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - And then we have the replica set followed by three pods that we created as part of the deployment.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 35 | **Type:** Concept
  - That's it for this lecture.
- **File:** `029_Deployments.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 36 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - We will now head over to a demo and I will see you in the next lecture.

### Services

- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this lecture we will discuss about Kubernetes services.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 2 | **Type:** Concept
  - Kubernetes services enable communication between various components within and outside of the application.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 3 | **Type:** Concept
  - Kubernetes services helps us connect applications together with other applications or users.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 4 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - For example, our application has groups of pods running various sections, such as a group for serving a front end load to users, another group for running back end processes, and a third group connecting to an external data source.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 5 | **Type:** Concept
  - It is services that enable connectivity between these groups of pods.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 6 | **Type:** Concept
  - Services enable the front end application to be made available to end users.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 7 | **Type:** Concept
  - It helps communication between backend and frontend pods and helps in establishing connectivity to an external data source.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 8 | **Type:** Concept
  - Thus, services enable loose coupling between microservices in our application.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 9 | **Type:** Concept
  - Let's take a look at one use case of services.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 10 | **Type:** Concept
  - So far we talked about how pods communicate with each other through internal networking.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 11 | **Type:** Concept
  - Let's look at some other aspects of networking in this lecture.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 12 | **Type:** Concept
  - Let's start with external communication.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 13 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So we deployed our pod having a web application running on it.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 14 | **Type:** Concept
  - How do we as an external user access the web page?
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 15 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - First of all let's look at the existing setup.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 16 | **Type:** Concept
  - The Kubernetes node has an IP address and that is 192.168.1.2.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 17 | **Type:** Concept
  - My laptop is on the same network as well, so it has an IP address 192.168.1.1.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 18 | **Type:** Concept
  - The internal Pod network is in the range 10.244.0.0, and the pod has an IP 10.244.0.2.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 19 | **Type:** Concept
  - Clearly I cannot ping or access the pod at address 244.0.2 as it's in a separate network.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 20 | **Type:** Concept
  - So what are the options to see the web page?
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 21 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - First, if we were to SSH into the Kubernetes node at 192.168.1.2, from the node, we would be able to access the pods web page by doing a curl, or if the node has a GUI, we would fire up a browser and see the web page in a browser following the HTT...
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 22 | **Type:** Concept
  - But this is from inside the Kubernetes node, and that's not what I really want.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 23 | **Type:** Concept
  - I want to be able to access the web server from my own laptop, without having to SSH into the node, and simply by accessing the IP of the Kubernetes node.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 24 | **Type:** Concept
  - So we need something in the middle to help us map requests to the node from our laptop, through the node to the pod running the web container.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 25 | **Type:** Concept
  - This is where the Kubernetes service comes into play.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 26 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The Kubernetes service is an object just like pods, replica sets, or deployments that we worked with before.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 27 | **Type:** Concept
  - One of its use cases is to listen to a port on the node, and forward requests on that port to a port on the pod running the web application.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 28 | **Type:** Concept
  - This type of service is known as a node port service, because the service listens to a port on the node and forward requests to the pods.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 29 | **Type:** Concept
  - There are other kinds of services available which we will now discuss.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 30 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The first one is what we discussed already.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 31 | **Type:** Concept
  - Node port where the service makes an internal pod accessible on a port on the node.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 32 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The second is cluster IP and in this case the service creates a virtual IP inside the cluster to enable communication between different services, such as a set of front end servers to a set of back end backend servers.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 33 | **Type:** Concept
  - The third type is a load balancer, where it provisions a load balancer for our application in supported cloud providers.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 34 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - A good example of that would be to distribute load across the different web servers in your front end tier.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 35 | **Type:** Concept
  - We will now look at each of these in a bit more detail along with some demos.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 36 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this lecture we will discuss about the Nodeport Kubernetes service.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 37 | **Type:** Concept
  - Getting back to Nodeport a few slides back, we discussed about external access to the application.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 38 | **Type:** Concept
  - We said that a service can help us by mapping a port on the node to a port on the pod.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 39 | **Type:** Concept
  - Let's take a closer look at the service.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 40 | **Type:** Concept
  - If you look at it, there are three ports involved.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 41 | **Type:** Concept
  - The port on the pod where the actual web server is running is 80, and it is referred to as the target port because that is where the service forwards the request to the second port is the port on the service itself.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 42 | **Type:** Concept
  - It is simply referred to as the port.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 43 | **Type:** Concept
  - Remember these terms are from the viewpoint of the service.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 44 | **Type:** Concept
  - The service is in fact like a virtual server inside the node.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 45 | **Type:** Concept
  - Inside the cluster.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 46 | **Type:** Concept
  - It has its own IP address, and that IP address is called the cluster IP of the service.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 47 | **Type:** Concept
  - And finally we have the port on the node itself which we use to access the web server externally.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 48 | **Type:** Concept
  - And that is known as the node port.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 49 | **Type:** Concept
  - As you can see it is set to 30,008.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 50 | **Type:** Concept
  - That is because node ports can only be in a valid range, which by default is from 30,000 to 32,767.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 51 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Let's now look at how to create the service.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 52 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Just like how we created a deployment replica set or pod in the past.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 53 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - We will use a definition file to create a service.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 54 | **Type:** Concept
  - The high level structure of the file remains the same as before.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 55 | **Type:** Concept
  - We have the API version kind, metadata and spec sections.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 56 | **Type:** Concept
  - The API version is going to be v1.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 57 | **Type:** Concept
  - The kind is of course service.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 58 | **Type:** Concept
  - The metadata will have a name and that will be the name of the service.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 59 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - It can have labels, but we don't need that for now.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 60 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Next we have spec and as always, this is the most crucial part of the file as this is where we will be defining the actual services.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 61 | **Type:** Concept
  - And this is the part of a definition file that differs between different objects in the spec section of a service.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 62 | **Type:** Concept
  - We have type and ports.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 63 | **Type:** Concept
  - The type refers to the type of service we are creating.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 64 | **Type:** Concept
  - As discussed before, it could be cluster, IP node, port or load balancer.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 65 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this case, since we are creating a node port, we will set it as node port.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 66 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The next part of a spec is ports.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 67 | **Type:** Concept
  - This is where we input information regarding what we discussed on the left side of the screen.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 68 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The first type of port is the target port, which we will set to 80.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 69 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The next one is simply port, which is the port on the service object.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 70 | **Type:** Concept
  - And we will set that to 80 as well.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 71 | **Type:** Concept
  - The third is node port, which we will set to 30,008 or any number in the valid range.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 72 | **Type:** Concept
  - Remember that out of these the only mandatory field is port.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 73 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - If you don't provide a target port, it is assumed to be the same as port.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 74 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - And if you don't provide a node port, a free port in the valid range between 30,000 and 32,767 is automatically allocated.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 75 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Also note that ports is an array, so note the dash under the ports section that indicate the first element in the array.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 76 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - You can have multiple such port mappings within a single service.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 77 | **Type:** Concept
  - So we have all the information in.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 78 | **Type:** Concept
  - But something is really missing.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 79 | **Type:** Concept
  - There is nothing here in the definition file that connects the service to the pod.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 80 | **Type:** Concept
  - We have simply specified the target port, but we didn't mention the target port on which pod.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 81 | **Type:** Concept
  - There could be hundreds of other pods with web services running on port 80.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 82 | **Type:** Concept
  - So how do we do that?
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 83 | **Type:** Concept
  - As we did with the replica sets previously, and a technique that you will see very often in Kubernetes.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 84 | **Type:** Concept
  - We will use labels and selectors to link these together.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 85 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - We know that the pod was created with a label.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 86 | **Type:** Concept
  - We need to bring that label into the service definition file.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 87 | **Type:** Concept
  - So we have a new property in the specs section and that is called selector.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 88 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Just like in a replica set and deployment definition, files under the selector provide a list of labels to identify the pod.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 89 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - For this, refer to the pod definition file used to create the pod.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 90 | **Type:** Concept
  - Pull the labels from the pod definition file and place it under the selector section.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 91 | **Type:** Concept
  - This links the service to the pod.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 92 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Once done, create the service using the kube control.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 93 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Create command and input the service definition file and there you have the service created.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 94 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - To see the created service, run the kube control.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 95 | **Type:** Concept
  - Get service's command that lists the service, the cluster IP and the mapped ports.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 96 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The type is node port as we created and the port on the node is set to 30,008, because that's the port that we specified in the definition file.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 97 | **Type:** Concept
  - We can now use this port to access the web service using Curl or a web browser.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 98 | **Type:** Concept
  - So curl to 1921681.2, which is the IP of the node.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 99 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - And then I use the port 30,008 to access the web server.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 100 | **Type:** Concept
  - So far we talked about a service mapped to a single pod, but that's not the case all the time.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 101 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - What do you do when you have multiple pods in a production environment?
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 102 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - You have multiple instances of your web application running for high availability and load balancing purposes.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 103 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - In this case, we have multiple similar pods running our web application.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 104 | **Type:** Concept
  - They all have the same labels with a key app and set to a value of my app.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 105 | **Type:** Concept
  - The same label is used as a selector during the creation of the service.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 106 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So when the service is created, it looks for a matching pod with the label and finds three of them.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 107 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The service then automatically selects all the three pods as endpoints to forward the external requests coming from the user.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 108 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - You don't have to do any additional configuration to make this happen.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 109 | **Type:** Concept
  - And if you're wondering what algorithm it uses to balance the load across the three different pods, it uses a random algorithm.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 110 | **Type:** Concept
  - Thus, the service acts as a built in load balancer to distribute load across different pods.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 111 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - And finally, let's look at what happens when the pods are distributed across multiple nodes.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 112 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this case, we have the web application on pods on separate nodes in the cluster.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 113 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - When we create a service without us having to do any additional configuration, Kubernetes automatically creates a service that spans across all the nodes in the cluster and maps the target port to the same node port on all the nodes in the cluster.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 114 | **Type:** Concept
  - This way, you can access your application using the IP of any node in the cluster and using the same port number, which in this case is 30,008.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 115 | **Type:** Concept
  - As you can see, using the IP of any of these nodes, and I'm trying to curl to the same port and the same port is made available on all the nodes part of the cluster.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 116 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - To summarize, in any case, whether it be a single pod on a single node, multiple pods on a single node, or multiple pods on multiple nodes, the service is created exactly the same without you having to do any additional steps during the service cr...
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 117 | **Type:** Concept
  - When pods are removed or added, the service is automatically updated, making it highly flexible and adaptive.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 118 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Once created, you won't typically have to make any additional configuration changes.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 119 | **Type:** Concept
  - That's it for this lecture.
- **File:** `033_Services.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 120 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Head over to the demo and I will see you in the next lecture.

### Services Cluster IP

- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this lecture we will discuss about the Kubernetes Service cluster IP.

  - You may have a number of pods running a front end web server, another set of pods running a back end server, a set of pods running a key value store like Redis, and another set of pods.

  - Maybe running a persistent database like MySQL.

- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 8 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - These pods can go down any time, and new pods are created all the time.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 9 | **Type:** Concept
  - And so you cannot rely on these IP addresses for internal communication between the application.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 10 | **Type:** Implementation Step

  - And who makes that decision?
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 13 | **Type:** Concept
  - A Kubernetes service can help us group the pods together, and provide a single interface to access the pods in a group.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 14 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - For example, a service created for the backend pods will help group all the backend pods together and provide a single interface for other pods to access this service.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 15 | **Type:** Concept
  - The requests are forwarded to one of the pods under the service randomly.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 16 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Similarly, create additional services for Redis and allow the backend pods to access the Redis systems through the service.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 17 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - This enables us to easily and effectively deploy a microservices based application on Kubernetes cluster.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 18 | **Type:** Concept
  - Each layer can now scale or move as required without impacting communication between the various services.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 19 | **Type:** Best Practice
  - Each service gets an IP and name assigned to it inside the cluster, and that is the name that should be used by other paths to access the service.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 20 | **Type:** Concept
  - This type of service is known as cluster IP.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 21 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - To create such a service, as always, use a definition file in the service definition file.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 22 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - First use the default template which has API version kind metadata and spec.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 23 | **Type:** Concept
  - The API version is v1, kind is service and we will give a name to our service.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 24 | **Type:** Concept
  - We will call it backend.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 25 | **Type:** Concept
  - Under specification we have type and ports.
- **File:** `034_Services Cluster IP.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 26 | **Type:** Concept
  - The type is cluster IP.

  - Uh, there.

### Namespaces

- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Concept
  - Hello and welcome to this lecture.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 2 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this lecture we will discuss about namespaces in Kubernetes.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 3 | **Type:** Concept
  - Let us begin with an analogy.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 4 | **Type:** Concept
  - There are two boys named Mark to differentiate them from each other.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 5 | **Type:** Concept
  - We call them by their last names, Smith and Williams.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 6 | **Type:** Concept
  - They come from different houses, of course, the Smiths and the Williams.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 7 | **Type:** Concept
  - There are other members in the house.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 8 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The individuals within the house address each other simply by their first names.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 9 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - For example, the father addresses Mark simply as Mark.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 10 | **Type:** Concept
  - However, if the father wishes to address the mark in the other house, he would use the full name.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 11 | **Type:** Concept
  - Someone outside of these houses would also use the full name to refer to the boys or anyone within these houses.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 12 | **Type:** Concept
  - Each of these houses have their own set of rules that defines who does what.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 13 | **Type:** Concept
  - Each of these houses have their own set of resources that they can consume.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 14 | **Type:** Concept
  - Now let's get back to Kubernetes.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 15 | **Type:** Concept
  - These houses correspond to namespaces in Kubernetes.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 16 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So far in this course, we've created objects such as pods, deployments, and services in our cluster.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 17 | **Type:** Concept
  - Whatever we have been doing, we have been doing within a namespace.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 18 | **Type:** Concept
  - We were inside a house all this while.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 19 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - This namespace is known as the default namespace, and it is created automatically by Kubernetes.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 20 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - When the cluster is first set up, Kubernetes creates a set of pods and services for its internal purpose, such as those required by the networking solution, the DNS service, etc. to isolate these from the user and to prevent you from accidentally ...
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 21 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - A third namespace created by Kubernetes automatically is called kube public.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 22 | **Type:** Best Practice
  - This is where resources that should be made available to all users are created.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 23 | **Type:** Best Practice
  - If your environment is small or you're learning and playing around with a small cluster, you shouldn't really have to worry about namespaces.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 24 | **Type:** Concept
  - You could continue to work in the default namespace.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 25 | **Type:** Concept
  - However, as of when you grow and use a Kubernetes cluster for enterprise or production purposes, you may want to consider the use of namespaces.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 26 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - You can create your own namespaces as well.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 27 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - For example, if you wanted to use the same cluster for both dev and production environment, but at the same time isolate the resources between them, you can create a different namespace for each of them.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 28 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - That way, while working in the dev environment, you don't accidentally modify resources in production.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 29 | **Type:** Concept
  - Each of these namespaces can have its own set of policies that define who can do what.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 30 | **Type:** Concept
  - You can also assign quota of resources to each of these namespaces.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 31 | **Type:** Concept
  - That way, each namespace is guaranteed a certain amount and does not use more than its allowed limit.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 32 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Going back to the default namespace that we have been working on, just like how the members within a house refer to each other by their first names, the resources within a namespace can refer to each other simply by their names.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 33 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this case, the webapp pod can reach the DB service simply using the hostname db service.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 34 | **Type:** Concept
  - If required, the webapp pod can reach a service in another namespace as well.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 35 | **Type:** Concept
  - For this, you must append the name of the namespace to the name of the service.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 36 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - For example, for the web pod in the default namespace to connect to the database in the dev environment or namespace, use the service name dot namespace svc cluster dot local format that would be DB service, dot dev, dot svc cluster local.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 37 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - You're able to do this because when the service is created, a DNS entry is added automatically in this format.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 38 | **Type:** Concept
  - Looking closely at the DNS name of the service, the last part, cluster dot local, is the default domain name of the Kubernetes cluster.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 39 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - SVC is the subdomain for service, followed by the namespace and then the name of the service itself.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 40 | **Type:** Operational Insight
  - Let us now look at some of the operational aspects of namespaces.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 41 | **Type:** Concept
  - Let us start with a kube control commands.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 42 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - For example, this command is used to list all the pods, but it only lists the pods in the default namespace.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 43 | **Type:** Concept
  - To list pods in another namespace, use the namespace option in the command along with the name of the namespace, in this case Kube system.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 44 | **Type:** Concept
  - Here I have a Pod definition file.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 45 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - When you create a pod using this file, the pod is created in the default namespace.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 46 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - To create the pod in another namespace, use the namespace option.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 47 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - If you want to make sure that this pod gets created in the dev environment all the time, even if you don't specify the namespace in the command line, you can move the namespace definition into the pod definition file like this.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 48 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Under the metadata section, this is a good way to ensure your resources are always created in the same namespace.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 49 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So how do you create a new namespace?
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 50 | **Type:** Concept
  - Like any other object, use a namespace definition file.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 51 | **Type:** Concept
  - The API version is v1, kind is namespace, and under metadata specify the name.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 52 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this case, dev.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 53 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Run the cube control create command to create the namespace.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 54 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Another way to create a namespace is by simply running the command cube control create namespace followed by the name of the namespace.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 55 | **Type:** Concept
  - Now say we are working in three namespaces, as we discussed before.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 56 | **Type:** Concept
  - By default we are in the default namespace, which is why we can see the resources inside the default namespace using the kube control get pods command.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 57 | **Type:** Concept
  - And to view those in the dev namespace, we have to use the namespace option.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 58 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - But what if we want to switch to the dev namespace permanently, so that we don't have to specify the namespace option anymore?
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 59 | **Type:** Concept
  - Well, in that case, use the kube control config command to set the namespace in the current context to dev.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 60 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - You can then simply run the kube control get pods command without the namespace option to list pods in the dev environment, but you will need to specify the option for other environments such as default or prod.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 61 | **Type:** Concept
  - Similarly, you can switch to the prod namespace the same way.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 62 | **Type:** Concept
  - Finally, to view pods in all namespaces, use the all namespaces option in the command.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 63 | **Type:** Concept
  - This will list all the pods in all of the namespaces, taking a closer look at the command.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 64 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - This command first identifies the current context and then sets the namespace to the desired one for that current context.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 65 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - Well, contexts are used to manage multiple clusters and multiple environments from the same management system.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 66 | **Type:** Concept
  - It is a totally separate topic to discuss and requires its own lecture, so we will discuss context in another lecture.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 67 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - To limit resources in a namespace, create a resource quota to create one.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 68 | **Type:** Concept
  - Start with a definition file for resource quota.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 69 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Specify the namespace for which you want to create the quota and then under spec provide your limits such as ten pods, ten CPU units, ten GB bytes of memory, etc..
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 70 | **Type:** Concept
  - Well, that's it for this lecture.
- **File:** `038_Namespaces.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 71 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Head over to the Coding exercises section and practice working with namespaces and I will see you in the next lecture.

### Imperative vs Declarative

- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this lecture, we will discuss about imperative and declarative approaches in Kubernetes.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 2 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - Towards the end of this lecture, we will talk about some tips that you can use in the exam.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 3 | **Type:** Concept
  - So far, we have seen different ways of creating and managing objects in Kubernetes.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 4 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - We created objects directly by running commands as well as using object configuration files.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 5 | **Type:** Concept
  - Now, in the Infrastructure as Code world, there are different approaches in managing the infrastructure, and they're classified into imperative and declarative approaches.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 6 | **Type:** Concept
  - Let's understand these with an analogy.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 7 | **Type:** Concept
  - Let's say you wanna visit a friend's house located at Street B.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 8 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Now, in the past, you would hire a taxi and give step-by-step instructions to the driver on how to reach the destination, like take right to street B, then take left to go to street C, and then take another left, and then right to go to Street D a...
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 9 | **Type:** Concept
  - Specifying what to do and how to do more importantly, is the imperative approach.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 10 | **Type:** Concept
  - On the other hand, today when you book a cab, say through Uber, you just specify the final destination, like drive to Tom's house, and this is the declarative approach.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 11 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - In this case, we're not giving step-by-step instructions.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 12 | **Type:** Concept
  - Instead, we're just specifying the final destination.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 13 | **Type:** Concept
  - We're declaring the final destination, and the system figures out the right path to reach the destination.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 14 | **Type:** Concept
  - Specifying what to do, not how to do is the declarative approach.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 15 | **Type:** Concept
  - So what's that got to do with what we are learning?
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 16 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - In the Infrastructure as Code world, an example of an imperative approach of provisioning infrastructure would be a set of instructions written step by step, such as provisioning a VM named a web server, installing the Nginx software on it, editin...
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 17 | **Type:** Concept
  - So here we're saying what is required and also how to get things done.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 18 | **Type:** Concept
  - In the declarative approach, we declare our requirements.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 19 | **Type:** Concept
  - For instance, all we say is that we need a VM by the name Web Server with the internet software on it, with a port set to 8080 and the path to the web files defined and where the source code of the application is stored, and everything that's need...
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 20 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - You don't have to provide step-by-step instructions.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 21 | **Type:** Concept
  - Orchestration tools like Ansible, Puppet or Chef or Terraform fall into this category.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 22 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - In the imperative approach, what happens if the first time only half of the steps were executed?
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 23 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - What happens if you provide the same set of instructions again to complete the remaining steps?
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 24 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - To handle such situations, there will be many additional steps involved, such as checks to see if something already exists and taking an action based on the results of that check.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 25 | **Type:** Concept
  - For instance, while provisioning a VM, what would happen if a VM by the name Web Server already exists?
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 26 | **Type:** Concept
  - The same goes with creating a database or importing data.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 27 | **Type:** Best Practice
  - Should it fail or should it continue since the VM is already there?
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 28 | **Type:** Concept
  - What if we decide to upgrade the version of software to say Nginx 1.18 in the future?
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 29 | **Type:** Best Practice
  - It should be as simple as updating the version of Nginx in the configuration file, and the system should take care of the rest.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 30 | **Type:** Best Practice
  - Ideally, the system should be intelligent enough to know what has already been done and apply the necessary changes only.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 31 | **Type:** Concept
  - That's the declarative way of doing things.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 32 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - In the Kubernetes world, the imperative way of managing infrastructure is using commands like the kubectl run command to create a pod, the kubectl create deployment command to create a deployment, the kubectl expose command to create a service to ...
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 33 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - For scaling a deployment or replica set, use the kubectl scale command, and updating the image on a deployment, we use the kubectl set image command.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 34 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Now, we have also used object configuration files to manage objects such as creating an object using the kubectl create -f command with the F option to specify the object configuration file, and editing an object using the kubectl, replace command...
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 35 | **Type:** Concept
  - All of these are imperative approaches to managing objects in Kubernetes.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 36 | **Type:** Concept
  - We're saying exactly how to bring the infrastructure to our needs by creating, updating or deleting objects.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 37 | **Type:** Best Practice
  - The declarative approach would be to create a set of files that defines the expected state of the applications and services on a Kubernetes cluster, and with a single kubectl apply command, Kubernetes should be able to read the configuration files...
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 38 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So in the declarative approach, you will run the kubectl apply command for creating, updating or deleting an object.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 39 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The apply command will look at the existing configuration and figure out what changes need to be made to the system.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 40 | **Type:** Concept
  - So let's look at these in a bit more detail.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 41 | **Type:** Concept
  - Now, within the imperative approach, there are two ways.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 42 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The first is using imperative commands such as the run, create or expose commands to create new objects and the edit, scale and set commands to update existing objects.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 43 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - Now, these commands help in quickly creating or modifying objects as we don't have to deal with YAML files.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 44 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - And these are helpful during the certification exams.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 45 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - However, there are limited in functionality and will require forming long and complex commands for advanced use cases such as creating a multi-container pod or deployment.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 46 | **Type:** Concept
  - Secondly, these commands are run once and forgotten.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 47 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - They're only available in the session history of the user who ran these commands, so it's hard for another person to figure out how these objects were created.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 48 | **Type:** Concept
  - So it is hard to keep track of.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 49 | **Type:** Concept
  - And so it's difficult to work with these commands in large or complex environments.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 50 | **Type:** Concept
  - And that's where managing objects with object configuration files can help.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 51 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Creating object definition files or configuration files or manifest files, as it's also called, can help us write down exactly what we need the object to look like in a YAML format and use the kubectl create command to create the object.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 52 | **Type:** Concept
  - We now have the YAML file with us always, and it can be saved in a code repository like Git.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 53 | **Type:** Concept
  - We can put together a change review and approval process around these files so that a change made is reviewed and approved before it is applied to a production environment.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 54 | **Type:** Concept
  - In the future, if a change is to be made, for instance, editing the image name to another version, there are different ways to go about it.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 55 | **Type:** Concept
  - One way is to use the kubectl edit command and specify the object name.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 56 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So when this command is run, it opens a YAML definition file similar to the one you used to create the object, but with some additional fields such as the status fields that you see here, which are used to store the status of the pod.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 57 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - This is not the file you used to create the object.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 58 | **Type:** Concept
  - This is a similar pod definition file within the Kubernetes memory.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 59 | **Type:** Concept
  - You can make changes to this file and save and quit, and those changes will be applied to the live object.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 60 | **Type:** Comparison
  - However, note that there is a difference between the live object and the definition file that you have locally.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 61 | **Type:** Concept
  - The change you made using the kubectl edit command is not really recorded anywhere.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 62 | **Type:** Concept
  - After the change is applied, you're only left with your local definition file, which in fact has the old image name in it.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 63 | **Type:** Concept
  - In the future, say you or a teammate decide to make a change to this object unaware that a change was made using the kubectl edit command, when the new change is applied, the previous change to the image is lost.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 64 | **Type:** Concept
  - So you can use the kubectl edit command if you are making a change and you're sure that you're not going to rely on the object configuration file in the future.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 65 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - A better approach to that is to first edit the local version of the object configuration file with the required changes.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 66 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - That is by updating image name here, and then running the kubectl replace command to update the object.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 67 | **Type:** Concept
  - This way, going forward, the changes made are recorded and can be tracked as part of the change review process.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 68 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So at times you may want to completely delete and recreate objects.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 69 | **Type:** Concept
  - In such cases, you may run the same command, but with the force option like this.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 70 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Now, this is still the imperative approach because you're still instructed Kubernetes how to create or update these objects.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 71 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - First, you run the kubectl create command to create the object, and then you run the replace command to replace the object or delete command to delete the object.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 72 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - And what if you run the create command if the object already exists?
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 73 | **Type:** Troubleshooting
  - Well, then it would fail with an error that says the pod already exists.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 74 | **Type:** Best Practice
  - When you update an object, you should always make sure that the object exists first before running the replace command.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 75 | **Type:** Troubleshooting
  - If an object does not exist, the replace command fails with an error message.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 76 | **Type:** Concept
  - So the imperative approach is very taxing for you as an administrator as you must always be aware of the current configurations and perform checks to make sure that things are in place before making a change.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 77 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The declarative approach is where you use the same object configuration files that we've been working on, but instead of the create or replace commands, we use the kubectl apply command to manage objects.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 78 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The kubectl apply command is intelligent enough to create an object if it doesn't already exist.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 79 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - If there are multiple object configuration files as you would usually, then you may specify a directory as the path instead of a single file.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 80 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - That way, all the objects are created at once.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 81 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Now, when changes are to be made, we simply update the object configuration file and run the kubectl apply command again, and this time it knows that the object exists, and so it only updates the object with the new changes.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 82 | **Type:** Troubleshooting
  - So it never really throws an error that says the object already exists or the updates cannot be applied.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 83 | **Type:** Concept
  - It'll always figure out the right approach to updating the object.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 84 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So going forward, any changes made on the application, whether they're updating images or fields of existing configuration files or adding new configuration files altogether for new objects, all we do is simply update our local directory with the ...
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 85 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So we will discuss more about how the kubectl apply command works exactly in the backend in the next lecture.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 86 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - For now, let me give you some tips as part of the exam.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 87 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - So from an exam perspective, you could use the imperative approach to save time as much as possible.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 88 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - For example, if a question is to just create a pod or a deployment with a given image, then one of these imperative commands can help you achieve that quickly.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 89 | **Type:** Concept
  - So it's important to practice the imperative commands.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 90 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - If you need to edit a property of an existing object, then using the kubectl edit command may be the quickest way, right?
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 91 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - If you have a complex requirement for, say, for example, that requires multiple containers, environment variables, commands, init containers, et cetera, then using an object configuration file to create the object would be preferred.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 92 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - This way, if you see that you made a mistake, you can easily update the file and apply it again, and using the kubectl apply command in that case would be a better option.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 93 | **Type:** Concept
  - So for more details on the different approaches to managing a Kubernetes cluster, get yourself familiarized with the Kubernetes documentation pages, and in the upcoming lab exercises, try to use imperative approach in solving questions.
- **File:** `041_Imperative vs Declarative.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 94 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Well, I'll see you in the next lecture.

### Lab Solution - Imperative Commands (optional)

- **File:** `045_Lab Solution - Imperative Commands (optional).extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Concept
  - -: Okay, in this video we will go over the practice test for imperative commands.
- **File:** `045_Lab Solution - Imperative Commands (optional).extraction.md` | **Entry:** 2 | **Type:** Concept
  - So in this lab we'll get hands on practice with creating Kubernetes objects imperatively.
- **File:** `045_Lab Solution - Imperative Commands (optional).extraction.md` | **Entry:** 3 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - And this is more for, more to help with your exams.
- **File:** `045_Lab Solution - Imperative Commands (optional).extraction.md` | **Entry:** 4 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - So you must be very familiar with imperative commands while you're preparing for the exam.
- **File:** `045_Lab Solution - Imperative Commands (optional).extraction.md` | **Entry:** 5 | **Type:** Concept
  - Okay, so let's, let's proceed.
- **File:** `045_Lab Solution - Imperative Commands (optional).extraction.md` | **Entry:** 6 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So the first task is to deploy a pod named NGINX pod using the NGINX Alpine image.
- **File:** `045_Lab Solution - Imperative Commands (optional).extraction.md` | **Entry:** 7 | **Type:** Concept
  - So that we've done before, it's easy.

### Kubectl Apply Command

- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 1 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - In this lecture we will understand more about how the kubectl apply command works.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 2 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - In the previous lecture, we saw how a kubectl apply command can be used to manage objects in a declarative way.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 3 | **Type:** Concept
  - In this lecture, we will see a bit more about how the command works.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 4 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - Internally, the apply command takes into consideration the local configuration file, a live object definition on Kubernetes, and the last applied configuration before making a decision on what changes are to be made.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 5 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So when you run the apply command, if the object does not already exist, the object is created.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 6 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - When the object is created, an object configuration similar to what we created locally is created within Kubernetes, but with additional fields to store status of the object.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 7 | **Type:** Concept
  - This is the live configuration of the object on the Kubernetes cluster.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 8 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - This is how Kubernetes internally stores information about an object, no matter what approach you use to create the object, but when you use the kubectl apply command to create an object, it does something a bit more.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 9 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - The YAML version of the local object configuration file we wrote is converted to a JSON format, and it is then stored as the last applied configuration going forward for any updates to the object.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 10 | **Type:** Comparison
  - All the three are compared to identify what changes are to be made on the live object.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 11 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - For example, say when the nginx image is updated to 1.19 in our local file and we run the kubectl apply command.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 12 | **Type:** Comparison
  - This value is compared with the value in the live configuration, and if there is a difference, the live configuration is updated with the new value.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 13 | **Type:** Concept
  - After any change, the last applied JSON format is always updated to the latest so that it's always up to date.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 14 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So why do we then really need the last applied configuration?
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 15 | **Type:** Concept
  - Right.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 16 | **Type:** Exam Tip
  - So if a field was deleted say for example, the type label was deleted.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 17 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - And now when we run the kubectl apply command, we see that the last applied configuration had a label, but it's not present in the local configuration.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 18 | **Type:** Concept
  - This means that the field needs to be removed from the live configuration.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 19 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So if a field was present in the live configuration and not present in the local or the last applied configuration, then it will be left as is.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 20 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - But if a field is missing from the local file and it is present in the last applied configuration, so that means that in the previous step or whenever the last time we run the kubectl apply command, that particular field was there and it is now be...
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 21 | **Type:** Concept
  - So the last applied configuration helps us figure out what fields have been removed from the local file.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 22 | **Type:** Concept
  - Right.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 23 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So that field is then removed from the actual live configuration.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 24 | **Type:** Concept
  - What we just discussed is available for your reference in detail in the Kubernetes document pages.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 25 | **Type:** Concept
  - So follow this link to view that.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 26 | **Type:** Concept
  - Okay.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 27 | **Type:** Concept
  - So we saw the three sets of files, and we know that the local file is what's stored on our local system.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 28 | **Type:** Concept
  - The live object configuration is in the Kubernetes memory.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 29 | **Type:** Concept
  - But where is this JSON file that has the last applied configuration stored?
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 30 | **Type:** Concept
  - Well, it's stored on the live object configuration on the Kubernetes cluster itself as an annotation named a last applied configuration.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 31 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - So remember that this is only done when you use the apply command.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 32 | **Type:** Warning/Pitfall
  - The create or replace commands do not store the last applied configuration like this.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 33 | **Type:** Concept
  - So you must bear in mind not to mix the imperative and declarative approaches while managing the Kubernetes objects.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 34 | **Type:** Comparison
  - So once you use the apply command going forward, whenever a change is made, the apply command compares all three sections the local pod definition file, the live object configuration, and the last applied configuration stored within the live objec...
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 35 | **Type:** Concept
  - Well, that's it for this lecture.
- **File:** `046_Kubectl Apply Command.extraction.md` | **Entry:** 36 | **Type:** Implementation Step
  - I will see you in the next.
